Clay immediately activated the scanner in his optical implant and scanned the area for additional power signatures, identifying more than a dozen in seconds. He didn’t like navigating hostile terrain with the constant distraction of the HUD’s telemetry but conceded that stumbling and falling every few meters was preferable to letting the mines cook him instantly with a blast of superheated ionized gas. 
He switched the implant’s sensor to thermal. As expected, it wasn’t sensitive enough to pick up the party through thick terrain in this climate. He did, however, detect a faint glow off to his right. It was approximately the size and shape of a human body, though far warmer than it should have been. He stayed low to the ground as he crept in the general direction of the heat source, trying to remember what the mission briefing had said about indigenous fauna on Notosia. Could there be a warm-blooded predator that large on Notosia that still hunted and didn’t hibernate during the winter months?
He reached a thicket of trees and was shocked to see Prudence, still wearing nothing but her loose-fitting surplice. Her expression showed she was completely oblivious to the icy wind that should be ripping at her skin. Yet, something was disturbing her. Her eyes were puffy and bloodshot. Snot and tears had frozen on her face, and she seemed on the verge of breaking down. According to the sensor readings, her body temperature was just over 41 degrees.   She regarded him without surprise and slowly raised a finger to her lips. He forced himself to his feet and regarded her with a puzzled look on his face. She then pointed to her left and began walking silently in that direction. He followed suit. 
Before he caught up to her, there was a faint buzzing and the slightest yelp of surprise behind him. He whirled and saw a short young woman with intense blue hair staring up at the temple to his right. Her face was a mixture of surprise and confusion. He wanted to call out to her to join them when his implant detected and highlighted the wire-thin security laser placed waist-high that she had just walked through. Seconds later, she fell forward, face still expressionless, as her body split just above the hips into two distinct pieces.
Clay whirled toward Prudence, who continued to walk away on a path perpendicular to the temple. “What the hell is this?!” he hissed. “Why?!”
“Marnie?” A voice called quietly from the darkness behind her. The noise of the fall had attracted one of the other pilgrims. Clay turned and was about to start back to warn the man of the trip laser, but it was too late. The laser was powered down, and the man was already kneeling next to the remains of the girl. Half in shock, he was still attempting to wake her when he finally realized what had happened. He sprang to his feet, stumbled backward four steps, and vomited his guts into the snow. Then, with a cry that would no doubt be heard back at the encampment, he took off, half-running, half-stumbling down the hill back to the cove.
Prudence had not broken her stride, and Clay struggled to catch up to her, finally grabbing her by the arm and spinning her around until they were face to face.  “What the hell is going on?! You didn’t have to kill those people!”
Through frozen tears, the girl appeared genuinely shocked. “I didn’t. He did. You know what I am. Do you really think I’m capable of that?!”
“Please,” Clay pleaded. “I need to know what’s going on. What is all this? Some kind of sick game Klein is playing? What does he have on you that you could go along with it!?”
“Only the chosen, Alex.”  She pulled away from him and continued on the path she had been walking. She was leading them somewhere. Clay struggled to keep up. 
They walked a thin, barely noticeable trail in silence for over half a kilometer, Prudence occasionally pointing out mines or other traps just off the trail that posed no real danger as long as he stayed in her footsteps. As they rounded a thicket of low trees, her hand flew up, motioning him to stop as she closed her eyes and listened to something in the distance. Clay’s implant picked up the faintest heat source between the trees about thirty meters down the hill.
Prudence motioned for him to wait as she tiptoed off the trail and down the hill toward the arriving pilgrim. Two minutes later, she returned with a pretty blonde girl following closely behind.  The new girl’s combination of confusion and terror must have matched his own earlier.
“We’re the last two,” the girl confirmed. “You must be Alex. We were dying to meet you back at the camp, wanting to know how a newcomer was chosen almost immediately after arriving, but you seemed content to stay in your tent.” She bowed slightly. “I’m Annah.”
Clay barely acknowledged the girl but gently moved her into position between himself and Prudence. “Stay close, Annah. Try to step where she’s stepping. This place is littered with all kinds of nasty traps and snares.”
“I know,” she nodded, voice trembling. “I’ve seen them.”
Oblivious to the conversation, Prudence continued to lead them along the path. Clay was certain he would never have spotted it, or at least would have lost it long ago, if not for her guidance. She’d walked it many times before, possibly every time there was a pilgrimage. 
After approximately fifteen minutes, Clay was certain they were merely walking in circles, never seeming to get any closer to the lights of the temple residence above. As inconspicuously as he could, he reached into his jacket and retrieved the plaser from its shoulder holster. Then, gently tapping Annah on the arm, motioned for her to get behind him. She stared at him, confused for a moment, then went ghostly white upon seeing the weapon and stopped in her tracks.
Undeterred and focused on what had to be done, Clay raised the plaser and slowed his pace, allowing the sudden silence to stop Prudence when she was ten paces away and firmly in his sights.
“Alex, no,” she protested, holding her hands up to protest her innocence. “I promise, it’s not what you think.”
“What I think,” Clay said calmly, “is that you’re avoiding taking us to that temple. That leads to a few very unpleasant conclusions. You’re not trying to get us to inadvertently set off one of these traps, are you?”
Prudence shook her head. “If it wasn’t for me, you would have set off a dozen traps by now. There are far easier ways to dispose of you.”
“Then that means you’re waiting for something, or should I say, someone?”
“That would be my fault, Mr. Girard,” a voice boomed through the darkness from somewhere up the hill. There was a flicker of lights, and Markus Klein, accompanied by three armed guards, appeared in silhouette on a narrow ledge above them. “I’m afraid I was detained and unable to welcome you properly until now. Prudence was merely following my instructions, so there is no need to blame her. Now, why not be a decent fellow and put the gun down before you injure someone, or yourself?”
Clay thought for a moment. He was outgunned, out-positioned, and, against his better judgment, would endanger both Prudence and the civilian, Annah, should a firefight break out here and now. Though he had hoped to enter Klein’s compound unseen and alone, this confrontation was inevitable. The only difference was that he would now have to face it unarmed. He powered down the plaser and tossed it to Prudence, who caught it and somehow vanished it into the folds of her outfit. For the moment, he still had the stuncaps in the holster that formerly held the plaser. He could use them to get his gun back from Prudence before the guards searched him.
Annah was now ghost white and had begun to shake.  Prudence put an arm on her shoulder and spoke softly in her ear, then motioned for the two of them to follow her up a different path toward the temple entrance. Clay noted that two of the guards had already moved out of sight, and the third, who stood at Klein’s side, kept his rifle pointed down. When the three of them finally reached the summit, they stepped onto a wide path leading to a large wooden arch that marked the entrance to the compound. There, lit from behind in an almost divine effect, Klein stood, arms outstretched, a welcoming smile on his face. 
“Annah, my child, I am delighted that you have made it to us at last. I have heard a great deal about you from Prudence here. I can’t wait to learn more.”
The girl began sobbing instantly, mostly from relief.  Klein’s massive hand went under her chin and directed her eyes up to meet his. She smiled and lost herself somewhere between a bow and a kneel, finally stopping and watching Klein for a clue on how to proceed. He motioned for her to stand up and put a paternal arm around her. “I’ll have none of that,” he said. “Now, you must be freezing. Go with Prudence. She’ll find you quarters and some food. Get a good night’s sleep. We’ll talk at breakfast tomorrow.”
Annah smiled for the first time and giggled in relieved innocence. Prudence handed Klein Clay’s plaser and gently led the girl towards a door on one side of the courtyard. 
Klein regarded the firearm for a moment, offhandedly showed it to his guard, who merely smiled, still not raising his rifle to a ready position. Finally, Klein pocketed the weapon and turned back to Clay. “Bonai Saigo seems overly determined to find a new partner, Mr. Girard. There are easier ways to get an appointment with me if your interest is truly legitimate.”  He gestured Clay toward a different entrance to the central building, which, from the limited structural plans Ness had obtained, appeared to be his personal residence. “Do they always arm their corporate officers for negotiations?”
They entered through the doors into a large foyer adorned with plants, fountains, and impressive works of art, all of which centered on a green-gemmed statue of a robed woman at the entryway, her arms palm up in a gesture of welcome. Aside from the composition, which was, without a doubt, pure jade, Clay found the detail work itself to be unremarkable and not particularly valuable. “It’s standard practice, yes,” he offered. “I hope you aren’t offended.”
“Not at all, sir.”
The foyer led to a large living room. Three levels of balconies above, all living apartments, were capped by a domed skylight through which moonlight illuminated the entire house. In some of the darker corners, small globes glowed a dim amber, complementing the ambiance without distracting from it. The wall opposite the balconies was entirely transplas and offered access to the back garden and its clifftop view of the ocean. Nakano City shimmered on the horizon kilometers away in the distance.
“Breathtaking view, isn’t it?” Klein offered, then motioned Clay through a large set of wooden double doors and into a study. He gestured for Clay to take a seat on an overstuffed couch while he himself sat behind a huge wooden desk that was easily worth more than the rest of the furnishings in the room. Klein dismissed the guard, retrieved Clay’s plaser from his pocket, pretending to study it closely. 
“You’re very trusting, Mr. Klein,” Clay said.
Klein waved a hand, dismissing the conversation. “Bonai Saigo wouldn’t want me dead. They’d want a deal,” he said, then his face turned sinister. “Likewise, Division 5 can’t kill me until they have secured Keraunos.”



CHAPTER 12.	(KLEIN)
Clay kept his composure. He had already half expected Klein to figure out who he was and why he was there. In many ways, dispensing with the cover simplified things. “If you’re so sure I’m not here to make a business deal, then why am I still alive, Klein?”
Klein laughed, regarded Clay’s plaser, turned it over several times, and finally tossed it back to Clay. “We're all islands shouting lies to each other across seas of misunderstanding, Sean Clay. Give me a chance to prove it.”
Clay checked the weapon’s power cell. Unexpectedly, it was full. He powered it on and watched the charge load, then powered it off again. As near as he could tell, Klein hadn’t tampered with it in any way. “You expect to believe you really are a religious zealot who wants nothing but peace?” He debated pointing the gun at Klein’s head and demanding Keraunos, then remembered how Klein had shot the marshal on the Maguro without the slightest hesitation or emotion. He checked that Klein's prosthetic arm was at his side, holstered the weapon, and nodded an insincere thank you.
“I may give that appearance as a shortcut to understanding my philosophies, but I’ve never encouraged any kind of faith in deities or spiritual nonsense. I’m trying to impart personal strength and self-reliance first and foremost, and then a desire to serve and help our fellow man.”  Klein smirked. “Is there any part of this that you disagree with so far?”
Clay sat back and regarded Klein. “Mostly the cold-blooded killing part that you seem to enjoy with the fervor of a psychopath.”
“Psychopath!?” Klein furrowed his brow, dismissing the word. “I expected better insults from you.”  He thought for a moment. “True, I have no trepidations about killing, few people on this planet do, but I like to think I reserve that only for those who deserve it.”
“Those kids tonight?” 
"Ah, ‘do not let us mistake necessary evils for good,’” he turned to look out the window. “You are right. Three deaths. A Cerberus operative who was after Keraunos, a Dark Serpent assassin who was after me, and a man of questionable morals who, according to Prudence, had already assaulted and/or taken advantage of several of the girls in the camp. None of them had any real interest in joining us here at the temple. You’ll note I spared Mr. Nappal, who was generally harmless, but placed far too much faith in others, and too little in himself. Maybe he’ll find the strength to try again. If not, I consider it no great loss.”
“What about Annah?”
“A student of medicine and lover of music. Prudence has every faith in her. We think she will be a strong leader for us one day. Even if she decides not to join us, we will make her a formidable force in whatever profession she wishes to pursue.”
Clay resisted the urge to roll his eyes. “You can’t expect me to believe you set all this up as some sort of self-improvement school and ask only people to volunteer to be your front-line soldiers when they graduate.”
“Truth often suffers more by the heat of its defenders than from the arguments of its opposers, Mr. Clay.”  Klein steepled his fingers under his chin. “Conscripts rarely make good soldiers, and there are enough of those on this planet anyway. Jade Shield will never have the numbers or the ruthlessness of a Cerberus, but we will make up for this with intellect, wisdom, and temperament. No, I believe you can change more minds with ideas, with words, even with art, than you can with guns and bombs.”  He chuckled to himself. “The dead have difficulty learning new concepts, you see.  By that metric, all of these anti-government factions have been dead for decades. They have no idea what Jade Shield truly is. Our tactics are subversion under the nose of oppression. Our weapons are the quill and voice. Our battlefield is the black market. Ground zero is Norinaga University.” He held his hands up in triumph, and without raising his voice, added, “and we’re winning, sir.” 
“So, why do you need Keraunos if you’re an army of intellectuals who are so dead set against violence?”
Klein’s voice dropped half an octave, and his eyes became slits. “I don’t need Keraunos, Mr. Clay. You do.”
“In that case, seeing as how we’re not enemies, and you don’t really need it, you’ll just hand it over to me if I ask?” Clay gave his most charming smile.
Klein smiled back, laughed heartily, and pushed himself away from the desk. He pulled one of the hundreds of books off one of the shelves that lined the walls, and a small safe slid into view.  It opened with a wave of his hand.  From inside, he extracted a blue data rod, walked over to Clay, and placed it on the desk directly in front of him. Clay could make out the “Locke Industries” logo, embossed in gold on one side.
Clay leaned forward and picked up the Rod.  He thought of a dozen ways Klein could kill him right now as he turned it over slowly in his hands.
“It’s quite genuine, I assure you. At least it had better be. It cost me quite a fortune.” Klein chuckled.
“So, what is it?” Clay asked, frustrated.
Klein laughed harder. “Honestly, I was hoping you knew. To me, it is encrypted gibberish, and nothing more than a means to annoy both Prevo and Cerberus simultaneously.”
“Which one does your friend Obryn work for?  He didn’t impress me as one of yours.”
“A foolish alliance on my part.” Klein sneered and shook his head. “He’s something of an up-and-comer who had advance knowledge of Keraunos and Locke’s travel itinerary. I had a lot more faith in him back then than I do now. Rumor has it that he has been cozying up with anyone he can to get hold of that and making promises he can’t possibly keep. We parted ways as soon as we got back from Excipio station. I should have killed him then, but at least this way I decided to add his annoyance to Cerberus’ and Prevo’s by acquiring that directly from Talbot,” he chuckled, then added, “for considerably more than I think Locke was hoping to get out of it, I might add.”
“We think it’s a newtech weapon. We also think someone wants to manufacture it here, on Notosia, and manipulated Locke into coming here. You don’t have those kinds of capabilities. What were you hoping it was?”
“Locke was no fool, Clay. He should have known he was a dead man six different ways, whether he made a deal with one of the factions, the government, the syndicates, or even if he tried to return home. I at least offered him a chance at a long life.”
A shadow crossed over the moon in the window behind Klein. Clay’s heart sank. “If only you could say the same for us,” he muttered, quickly pocketing the datarod.
Klein looked confused and walked to the window. “What did you see?”
Clay walked up next to him and looked out at the sea, illuminated by the moon. “I’m not up on your local birdlife, but if that’s what it was, it had a wingspan of nearly three meters.”
“Nonsense,” Klein said, leaning forward to catch any sign of what Clay was talking about.
Seconds later, the shape appeared again. It was spirally descending over the estate. “That’s no bird. It’s a glider,” Clay gasped.
“A glider?  Who would be on a glider at this time of night?”
Clay moaned and pulled the plaser from its holster. “A particularly persistent ninja who stole that for Tachibana’s Shadow before Talbot used his leverage to force the girl to betray everyone and give it to you. I’d alert your guards. Also, if we get out of this alive, I’d have this place swept for transmitters. His arrival seconds after you pulled that out of the safe is too much of a coincidence.”
Klein walked to the desk and pressed a button. “Intruder on the compound. Guards to the roof and ground level.”  An alarm sounded in the distance, and outside the doors, Clay heard a half-dozen people shouting orders to each other.
“You don’t have any other way out of this room? A safe room?  A secret passage?”
“This is the safe room. There’s a passage up to the bedroom on the top floor. Not exactly the direction we want to be heading right now.” He typed a command on the desk’s holoterminal, and Clay heard seals engage on the large door at the front of the office. Klein gestured to the monitor, which showed the ninja, having already landed on the roof and dispatched the guards there, smashing open the skylight and swinging down to the balcony directly in front of the third-floor bedroom. Stopping only to incapacitate the lone guard who ran from the room to challenge him, Hēi Gēzi then leaped over the railing, disappearing from the camera view completely.
There was a clamor of shouted orders and gunfire in the living room outside, several of which sounded as if they had struck the large doors to the study. Eventually, shouts turned to screams of desperation and agony, then gradually subsided. There was only the faintest sound of scratching from directly on the other side of the study’s entrance. 
The seals hissed open again, and the door to the study clicked and slid back. Clay ducked behind the desk next to Klein and aimed his gun at the entrance. Hēi Gēzi stood in the doorway, sword in one hand and a hysterical guard acting as an improvisational shield in the other. The guard was missing his right arm at the elbow and was just taking his blood-soaked left hand from the door lock controls. The ninja pushed the guard through the doorway, slashed his neck with the sword, then crouched and darted for the room’s left wall.
Clay fired twice, wildly missing as Hēi Gēzi closed the distance, vaulted the desk, and landed flat-footed on the other side. He grabbed the gun and bent it forward, stripping it out of Clay’s hand. Klein attempted to grab the sword with his cybernetic arm, but the ninja was faster, twirling it with great dexterity as he stuck the point in Klein’s leg just above the knee. The big man lost his balance and fell backward, firing once with the dart launcher in his arm. Hēi Gēzi switched his killing lunge into a blocking sweep that deflected Klein’s dart. Clay slammed into him, sending both reeling. The ninja pivoted, flipped Clay so that he hit the desk square in the middle of his back, then spun, stopping when the flat of the sword was lying across Clay’s throat. Suddenly, the room was quiet.  Clay could hear Klein whimpering in the corner behind the desk, and his own heart pounding in his ears.
“Talbot is dead,” Clay said hastily. The ninja froze in his tracks. “Your girlfriend is about to be in a world of pain. I can help her. Why don’t we strike a bargain?”
Behind the mask and goggles, the ninja tilted his head as if sizing him up. For a moment, Clay thought he might even agree to a deal. In a flash, the sword was lifted and Hēi Gēzi’s hand turned, preparing to bring the butt down on Clay’s face. Clay blocked it reflexively this time, half-surprising himself when he succeeded.
“Look,” he said, leaping backward over the console and positioning himself on the opposite side. “Can you go with the right side for once? Between the two of you, the left temple is starting to get sore.”
The black-clad figure showed no sign of understanding what he was saying and planted a knee square into Clay’s abdomen. Before Clay could regain his balance, he was thrown over a chair and into a large marble pedestal, nearly knocking over the stone sculpture on top. He grabbed the sculpture and lunged forward, swinging it at the ninja’s head. Its unexpected weight and the earlier kick to his stomach caused him to stumble with the weight of it.
The ninja merely leaned backward, neatly dodged the swing, and followed up with a backflip that put two feet into Clay’s chin.  He landed on his feet again, a split second later. Clay, laying on the ground, momentarily oblivious as to how he got there, opened his eyes to find the point of a sword centimeters from his face.  
“Listen to me,” Clay insisted. “I’ve got the last five doses of Shard on this entire planet and the possible means to make more off-world. If you kill me, that knowledge goes with me.”
Hēi Gēzi said nothing but held his left hand out, sword still pointing directly between Clay’s eyes.
“Yeah, okay, you’re younger, faster, and better trained than me, and you’ve got the pointy thing there.”  He slowly reached into his jacket pocket beneath his holster. “But you know what I have?”  He retrieved a small cylindrical object and placed it in the ninja’s hand, then rolled onto his stomach, curling into a fetal position.
The stuncap went off instantly, sending Hēi Gēzi backward three meters and into the wall. The ninja landed in a crumpled heap, writhing in pain.
“Brains!” Clay yelled as he stood up and bolted for Klein, ignoring his slightly blurred vision and ringing ears. “Klein, we’ve got to move!”
“Ask for me tomorrow, and you shall find me a grave man.” The big man was panting and winced in pain. “I’ll be fine, but right now I’ll only slow you down.  Take the datarod and go!”
Clay kept the stunned ninja in the corner of his eye, still down for now. “How do I get to your roof?”
Klein nodded. “Third floor utility closet.  There’s a ladder.”
Clay put a hand on the big man’s shoulder, then turned and scurried out of the study. He crossed the wide parlor underneath the bedroom balcony, broken glass from the shattered skylight reflecting moonlight like stars. The room was eerily silent. He decided to take a chance and ran up the stairs, watching the hallway below for Hēi Gēzi. When he reached the third floor, he opened all the doors and compartments until he found the ladder leading to the roof. He quickly darted up, opened the access hatch at the end, and climbed out.
Two nearly full moons bathed the roof in moonlight, allowing him to maneuver confidently. After a few seconds of searching, he found what he was looking for. He hefted the weighty gliderpack over his back and slid his arms through the control sleeves. It took longer than he’d hoped to find the power-up switch and adjust the fittings -- long enough for a familiar figure to emerge from the shadows, marching toward him with purpose.
Clay backed away, still attempting to adjust the chest piece so he didn’t slip out of the pack as soon as he was airborne. Hēi Gēzi grabbed his shoulder with his left hand and hit him with a palm strike under the chin with his right. It was a simple attack and now required both hands to position the target. 
“So, the stuncap took something out of you. Normally, I would never resort to a cheap tactic like that, but it’s nice to know you’re mortal after all.”  
Though he was seeing stars, Clay decided to press his luck and charged back at the ninja, knocking them both over and to the ground. He managed to haul himself upright, put both fists together, and pounded them into Hēi Gēzi’s face as hard as he could. Unfortunately, his opponent, still faster, deflected them and tilted his head, only catching a glancing blow before using Clay’s momentum to flip him off and onto his back.
Clay was still catching his breath when the Ninja silently pounced on him, planting a knee squarely in his groin and a fist in his solar plexus.
Completely disoriented, Clay kicked wildly, knocking Hēi Gēzi off of him and onto his side. He quickly stumbled to his feet and aimed his gun squarely at the masked man, at the same time making sure he was far from any potential hand or fist attacks. The ninja froze, watching him, making no attempt to stand up.  
“Okay, look,” Clay said, panting for air. “I’ve got your toys, I’ve got Keraunos, I’ve got the Shard. I’ve won. Maybe your stupid ninja code requires death before the dishonor of admitting defeat, but at least tell the girl I’m willing to save her.” 
Hēi Gēzi still did not move. He lay on his side and regarded Clay. It might have been defeat, curiosity, or even amazement. There was no way to tell beyond the featureless mask and goggles.
“You know what, fuck it. You’ve really pissed me off. I’ll tell her myself.”  He pulled the trigger of the gun a split second after the shuriken embedded itself in his wrist, causing the shot to veer wildly. At his wits’ end, Clay threw the gun as hard as he could at the ninja’s head, also missing completely, ran for the cliff-side edge of the roof, and leaped off into the night. 
He waited five seconds before hitting the deploy button that extended the glider’s wings. With an ominous “whump,” he heard the struts and fabric deploy, and instantly, his free-fall velocity became lateral momentum. He sailed out over the ocean, located the amphib where Ness was waiting to retrieve him, and banked right, putting himself on a direct course. He cursed himself for letting his rage unbalance him to the point that he had nearly gotten himself killed just now, simply to show up an upstart assassin, and he was the only one who knew how dangerously close he had just come to splattering himself on the cliffside if he’d misunderstood the gliderpack’s controls.
At least one primary objective was complete.  He finally had Keraunos. He patted his jacket where he had stuck the data rod and found it empty.

CHAPTER 13.	(FONTAINE)
He couldn’t believe he had come so close. Keraunos was in his hands. Hēi Gēzi was in his sights, and Ursza would either come with him or be dead in a matter of days. He didn’t enjoy that last idea, but he decided he could live with a successful mission. Now, he was starting from scratch again. 
He sat in the back of the amphib while Ness piloted them through the darkness back to Nakano City.  The sea air had a smell and taste unlike anything he was familiar with on Earth.  Not clean, but at least more pleasant than Tahmo Island had been two days before. Ness kept one eye on the controls and handed him her datapad, scowling.  “While you’re out playing in the woods with the kids, all hell is breaking loose in the city.” Her expression was neutral, but not quite detached. 
He grabbed the pad and started scrolling through newsfeeds -- street gangs, criminal organizations, resistance fighters. He put the pad down and began rubbing his eyes. His head was still throbbing from the beating he’d taken from the Ninja, to say nothing of being within a meter of a stuncap when it went off. Now, after twenty minutes of bobbing over waves in an amphib, he was slowly adding nausea to his list of ailments. “Can you give me headlines? It’s been a rough night.”
“With Shard about to be a very limited commodity, the factions who kept a stockpile are sitting on a goldmine, while the others are facing disaster with a percentage of their most desirable recruits now on a ticking clock before withdrawal starts. It’s accelerating everything.”
“Couldn’t happen to a nicer group of people, as far as I’m concerned.”
“The correction vendors are making their money at least. I’ve never seen so many variant truths out there.”  Ness laughed. “My last correction stated that Tachibana’s Shadow was now responsible for the starport bombing. This put a crimp in their plans to go to war with Dark Serpent, since now the Avengers of Red Mountain and Gi-no Gunzei have thrown in against them.”
“Over a starport bombing?  Why would Tachibana’s Shadow bomb a starport?”
Ness looked up.  Six drones, noticeable only by the reflection of moonlight on their silvery surface, flew overhead towards Klein’s island compound.  She didn’t speak until they lost sight of them in the darkness.
“The practical answer is: because Mother Eye wants them removed from the board, probably so their Shard addicted recruits will go running to the government and spill all that they know about the other factions in exchange for leniency and a fix.”
Clay switched to rubbing at his temples. “While the local civics lesson is extremely entertaining, if frustrating, the mission is to find that Ninja and get Keraunos. For that, we need to use the Shard that I’ve got to get Ursza to help us. The problem is finding her.”
“The easiest way to do that would be to ask someone who regularly works with her, you know, like Sem Talbot or Nils Hoefer.  Oh, wait, they’re indisposed now.”  She paused and pointed to her right.  Clay spotted another amphib with the assistance of his ocular implant.  There were six men on them.  Security team, he thought, not Tokusha.
He sneered. “Dre Berger’s alive, and I’m betting he’d also like a word with Ursza after she tried to pin Hoefer’s murder on him.”
“Yes, and he’d probably like a word with you for the same reason.”
“In which case, I won’t need to worry about not having an appointment to see him.”
“Clay, maybe he gave you the wrong impression when he was drooling over Nils Hoeffer more than he was over a yorohito on a yishi table, but you do realize he didn’t get to be manager of the Hotel Fontaine because of his skills as a businessman, right?  He might use you to fertilize the plants in his lobby.”
“He’s a basic opportunistic influence peddler.  Of all the odd people, strange vices, and crazy politics I’ve dealt with on this insane planet, Berger is the only one I even halfway understand.  I can handle him.”
#
Clay wandered into the first floor of the Hotel Fontaine, smiled politely at the holographic concierge, and proceeded straight to the lounge where Ursza had slipped him the yishi chit two days prior. He signaled for the bartender and ordered a bourbon and settled back to watch the newsfeeds, confident that he was on somebody’s radar by now, judging by the number of otherwise invisible surveillance cameras his ocular implant was picking up.
The newsfeed on the holodisplay behind the bar flickered. According to the story, the Eyes of Ball were being blamed for a home invasion and burglary of the island compound of Markus Klein, resulting in significant injury to Klein and two staffers, as well as the deaths of four security guards and the maiming of a third. There was no mention of what, if anything, was supposedly stolen.
“It’s awfully late to be out drinking, don’t you think?” a female voice asked from behind him.  Clay stood up and turned, not wanting to be caught flat-footed.
“Vivaine,” he said warmly.  “I was hoping I’d get to see you again.  I’m sorry I missed your show on the yishi table last night.”
Vivaine smiled. “Mmmmm. You missed a good one. They broke two of my ribs early, so breathing was excruciating. Then one of the younger boys got a real charge out of breaking my fingers one at a time. I’d never experienced that before. This younger generation can be very inventive.”
“So, a couple of hours on an autodoc and you’re back at work the next day?” He tried not to visibly wince and almost considered activating his anti-deception routines to keep his heart rate and pupil dilation in check, but decided against it when he realized Vivaine wasn’t paying attention to him but instead scanned the room, as if looking for someone.
“I have a guild-issued regen unit that is hospital-grade. Nothing but a bit of discoloration and some soreness today.”  She wiggled her fingers to demonstrate. Clay saw only the faintest bruising on the knuckles. “So, why are you here? Not enough action the other day?”
“I was hoping to get a word with your boss,” Clay said.
“Yes, I thought you might be.  That’s why I came over. You need to leave, now. He wants you dead, you know. Killing you himself would be a bonus.”
“Yes, that’s part of why I’m here. I thought we might be able to come to terms.”
“I find that a very unlikely possibility. Mr. Berger doesn’t do terms. He’s a very busy man. A man who is used to getting what he wants.”
“Exactly, and I believe I have something he wants,” Clay said confidently. 
Vivaine turned and looked him in the eyes, and for the first time, he saw fear on her face.  Seconds later, there was a cough behind his back, and a large man in a gray suit stepped between them.
“Mr. Gerard,” he said politely, “My name is Jager.  I am to escort you to Mr. Berger’s suite.”
Vivaine deflated instantly, finished her drink, and set it on the counter.  Clay took this as a cue to do the same. Jager quickly frisked Clay, removing his identicard, pocket terminal, and plaser.  He then led them out of the lounge, where two formally dressed guards led them to the elevator and up to the 89th floor, Jager cautiously taking position in the back of the car.
Clay resisted the urge to make a frivolous joke as the car carried the five of them up for what seemed like an unusually long ride.   When the doors finally parted, the pool deck and dining area were dimly lit and empty.  A bartender who appeared to be cleaning up after a rather busy night looked up, examined the group, and promptly went back to work.
The guards marched them into the same suite that, two days earlier, had held the yishi party, still immaculately decorated with the same marble statues, though furniture had now been brought back in.  When they entered the parlor, a large grand piano sat in the corner where Hoefer and Berger had been standing the night before.  He wasn’t sure if the table was the same.
They walked down the hall into the room where Hoefer had met his end.  Dre Berger was leaning against the desk, watching the doorway for their arrival, arms folded across his chest.  An overweight man with thinning white hair sat in a wingback chair to his left, sipping a clear drink in a wide-bottomed glass.  A third man, with a tangle of red hair, was standing in the window looking out over the city, hands clasped behind his back.  When the group entered, he turned around.  Clay recognized him instantly as Obryn, Klein’s traveling companion on the Maguro.
Jager crossed the room and immediately handed Berger Clay’s identicard, terminal, and sidearm.  Berger spent twenty seconds examining all three, keeping the gun in his hand and placing the other two items on his desk.
“Mr. Girard,” Berger said, not warmly, but without aggression, “infamous party crasher, murder suspect, horrible dresser. What brings you to my humble establishment again?  Would you like a drink?”  He gestured to a small liquor counter in the corner. “Jager, make the man a drink.”
Clay leaned forward in the chair. This was the “rapport-building part of the interrogation,” he decided.  It was best to lay the groundwork here before Berger lays down dominance. “My apologies for the other night, Mr. Berger. I understand you are no doubt angry about having Hoefer’s murder pinned on you like—”
“Angry?!” Hoefer laughed. “I thought it was hilarious, as did everyone else in the room. How could I logistically have shot Hoefer? For that matter, why would I shoot Hoefer?”
“I see, in that case—”
“Oh, I knew some of the shady stuff he was into,” Hoefer interrupted. “Beating girls, even if they were only yorohito, was the least of his sins. Still, he was one of my best customers, and he paid his bills. Well, that is up until somebody puts a plasma bolt in his face right here in my office, while the suite outside was full of very important people.”  
Jager handed Clay a tumbler with bourbon and ice.  Clay took the glass and rested his hand on the armrest of the chair, not daring to drink it.  Out of the corner of his eye, he watched Vivaine saunter over to the bar and make a drink for herself. She seemed unconcerned with the conversation.
“So, Mr. Girard, what would possess you to come back here, knowing I wouldn’t exactly be happy to see you?”
“Bonai Saigo sent me here to strike a partnership with Locke to develop a certain technology. Hoefer tried to steal the plans for that tech, but the girl, Venter, double-crossed him and has likely already sold them to another buyer. I need to get to her, find out where they are, and get them back.”
“And this is my concern? What made you think I would help you?”
“A business proposition,” Clay said calmly.
“It’s very hard to do business with a dead man.” Berger laughed at his joke and waved Clay's gun nonchalantly.  The large man in the chair opposite Clay rolled his eyes and shifted his weight, bored.
Vivaine crossed the room to Clay’s other side and leaned against the wall, watching him intently with an unsettling smile as she sipped on a long-stemmed glass of the ubiquitous green liqueur.  
Obryn straightened and walked to the other side of the room next to the large man in the chair. He was walking with a very noticeable limp. “Come off it, Berger, your business will be back to normal next week, you had no love for Hoefer, and you’re no killer. You’re not going to harm him, and he can read that all over your face.”
Clay smiled, nodding his head at Obryn’s leg.  “You appear to have had a rough night, Mr. Obryn.”
“Comes with the lifestyle, I’m afraid, but nothing I can’t handle, I assure you.” Obryn smiled, then nodded back to the man in the chair.
“Well, I hope the other guy fared worse, then.” 
“As you say,“ Obryn smiled coldly, then gestured to the large man seated opposite Clay.  “Alexandre Girard, this is Max van Rijn, head of another of those organizations your company is loath to do business with.”
Clay attempted to look uncomfortable. “Let me guess.  Eyes of Baal?”
“The Eyes?!” Van Rijn laughed heartily. “What do they care about off-world businessmen? They never leave Harba City. No, sir, I am the head of the Dark Serpent Cartel,” he attempted to make the name sound scary, but then dismissed this notion.
“Well, then, I’m honored. From what I’ve heard, you’re the new big player on this planet.”
He heard Vivaine stir behind him as she crossed the room, set her empty glass on the table in front of Clay, and moved to stand next to Obryn. 
Van Rijn scoffed. “And where did you hear that?”
Clay shrugged. “It was on the news. Avengers of Red Mountain and Gi-no Gunzei are backing you in your war against Tachibana’s Shadow.”
“Ah, Mother Eye, of course.” Van Rijn rolled his eyes. “We had no interest in Tachibana’s Shadow until Mother Eye suggested we were going to war with them.  We merely called in favors to get the protection of ARM and Gi-no Gunzei, in case the Shadow took the report seriously.  Later, we found out that Tachibana’s Shadow all but collapsed under their own infighting over Hoefer’s position. Their leaders went to a half-dozen other factions, their foot soldiers went to Cerberus.”
Obryn shook his head.  “Cerberus is not our concern—”
“Enough talk,” Berger half-shouted, still waving Clay’s gun around. “I’m more interested in what Mr. Girard here is offering in exchange for the girl.”  Finally, he set the gun down on the desk and sat down in an oversized chair.
Clay stared at him.  “Sem Talbot is dead. He was the sole source of Shard on this world. Sure, some of the bigger players have stockpiles, but eventually they’ll run out unless someone comes forth with a new source.  It could be leverage for someone--”
Vivaine slid her hand down the wall as she walked from Obryn to Van Rijn, caressing the back of his chair with an odd look on her face.  She looked up at Jager and winked.
“It’s a synthetic compound of his own making, not something you just stumble on by accident.  I’m certain Talbot took that secret to his grave,” Berger continued. 
“Nonetheless, I believe I have his process.  He wasn’t stupid, but he was arrogant enough to have it on a large holo-display in his office.”
Berger considered this, then shook his head. "Maybe you don't know how many people come to me every month claiming they've cracked the process and have the secret to making Shard. I'm not buying it. If you're so sure you've got something, take it back to Bonai Saigo and do it yourself."
“I have no interest in anything like that, I just want the girl. Deliver her, or at least tell me how I can get in touch with her. The formula is yours. Maybe it's legit, maybe not. The only thing I took from it was a favor, and I can always pay that back.”
Vivaine let out an insane little giggle and grinned wildly.  Something was suddenly very “off” with her, Clay decided. She was possibly high on something, judging by the look on her face.
Berger seemed to ponder Clay’s offer for a moment, then he walked back around his desk and sat down.  “Very generous. I believe I will pass.”
“Why? It’s a good deal, Berger.”
“It’s an excellent deal, except that several people are protecting her, I suspect.” 
“Who’s protecting her?” Clay kept his tone business-like.
Berger recoiled in confusion. “Who!?  Well, for one thing, a rather vicious martial artist and assassin is very protective of her.  After that, the government apparently wants her alive.  Why do you think the newsnets blamed Hoefer’s murder on you, the boring off-world businessman, instead of her, the mysterious operative who has worked for every faction on the planet at one point or another and currently keeps an assassin’s appointment calendar?”  He wrung his hands nervously.  Clay heard Jager shifting his weight behind him. “I’m just a hotel keeper who doesn’t take sides in that kind of stuff.  Hell, right now, ten factions that are likely to be at war tomorrow, rented rooms and gambled in my casino tonight. Why? Because I run a legit business and I plan to keep it that way.”
It was that exact instant when Vivaine chose to strike. As soon as she raised her left arm, Clay caught the glint of a steel blade in her hand, headed for Max Van Rijn’s neck.  He instantly stood up, kicked Van Rijn’s chair over and out of her striking range, and secured her wrist before Jager had even pulled his sidearm from its shoulder holster. Berger instantly recoiled in his chair and slid backward. Obryn stood upright and made two steps forward towards them, watching the situation carefully, still heavily favoring his right leg.
Vivaine looked at the blade in her hand, then at Clay.  Her face immediately turned to concern. “Don’t. Stay out of this,” she whispered, her voice almost a plea.  
The two struggled over the blade.  Clay pulled her arm down in an attempt to lock it behind her back, but she countered with a knee to his stomach, causing him to lose his grip long enough for her to twist and break the hold. She slashed out, barely missing his chest. Her eyes were wide, pupils dilated.  Her face had a contorted grin of pleasure.  She was breathing deeply and slowly, almost a sigh of contentment.
“Shoot her, you idiot!” Berger yelled at Jager, who stood frozen next to Clay, gun aimed at Vivaine the entire time.
“But … it’s Vivaine, boss,” Jager protested.
“I said shoot her!”
Vivaine charged Jager, slashing wildly.  He raised his gun, and she caught him with a deep gash that made him recoil. Clay stepped in behind her, hooked her knife arm with his, lifted the blade above her head, and planted a right cross on her jaw that, while it connected badly, certainly hurt.  She growled and grabbed the knife in both hands, attempting to break his hold.
The two stumbled backward. Vivaine brought the knife straight down, catching Clay’s bicep but missing his torso completely as he pivoted. He moved in, four hands now wrestling for the blade, and twisted her arms, trying to force her to drop the knife.  Vivaine only laughed, blood gushing from her mouth from the punch he’d thrown. Still refusing to drop the knife, no matter how hard he twisted.
She spat a mouthful of blood into his face and began kicking, screaming, and biting.  In the chaos, he felt her wrist slipping out of his grip. She was out of control and had left him no options. He pushed in again and twisted, felt her wrist break as the knife came free, and drove the blade straight up under her sternum.  There was a yelp of surprise, almost ecstasy, then her eyes went blank, and she crumpled to the floor.
Berger was beside himself, his desk chair against the far wall.  Van Rijm slowly pulled himself up from the floor and straightened his jacket.  Obryn, calm as ever, slowly limped back to his original spot, leaning on a cadenza. Jager was nursing his bleeding arm, his face ghostly white as he looked from Vivaine’s body to Berger.  Clay picked up Jager’s discarded sidearm from the floor and held it at his side, not sure whom to trust at this point.
Berger stood up slowly.  His jaw dropped, and his face conveyed shock and dismay. He looked at the bloodied, lifeless form of Vivaine, then at Clay.  “She was yorohito!” he exclaimed. “I’ve got her guild credentials. It should be impossible for her to hurt anyone.”
“The rumors are true,” Obryn whispered. “I’ve heard talk of this. They’ve figured out how to corrupt the conditioning. Turn the trigger from emotional or physical pleasure to pain and suffering.”
Berger shook his head. “You’re saying she enjoyed that the way she enjoyed giving men a rush in a yishi ceremony?”
Obryn nodded. “What better way to disguise the corruption? You think she was riding an endorphin high from the men’s arousal. She was actually getting it from witnessing the pain and violence.“
Clay set the knife down on Berger’s desk and grabbed his confiscated belongings. “Who? Who’s figured out how to corrupt yorohito?”
“Prevo,” Obryn said quietly.
Berger shook his head in disbelief. “Jager, go downstairs and find someone to clean up this mess, then kick all the yorohito out of the lounge, and tell Lukas not to let any yorohito in without running it by me first … wait … what am I saying.  Tell Lukas to do all that. You’re fired!”
Slowly, Jager set Clay’s personal effects he’d taken from the bar on the table, then turned and quietly left.
Berger nodded to Clay. “Just a businessman from Procyon, eh?  Obviously not.  Who do you work for then? Couldn’t be one of the factions, I know everybody there. Couldn’t be Prevo, she was his and tried to take your face off just now.  So, who’s your boss?  The Raven?  Tarek Saleh?  Hell, maybe you are one of them?”
Clay examined Jager’s dropped plaser and slipped it into his pocket.  “Look, Berger, you don’t want to be a Shard dealer, that’s fine, but I’ve got to find that girl.  Are you going to help me, or do you want to be on my bad side?”
“Hey,” Berger held up both hands. “I’d love to help you, but honestly, I run a hotel. There isn’t much call to hire ninja assassins. If anyone inquired, I would refer them to any faction head who has done business with her.  Sorry, but that’s all I can give you.”
Clay glared at Van  Rijm, who merely smiled.  “I owe you no small debt for saving my life, my friend, but alas, I have no idea how to contact her either.  In the past, she has always seemed to know when I needed such services, and simply appeared.”
“I heard a rumor that Talbot rented an apartment for her in Low Town, down on the eastern shore,” Obryn offered.
“That’s less than helpful,” Van Rijm said, “there are thousands of apartments in Low Town, and the people who live there are more likely to skin him alive for being a suspected off-worlder or Tokusha than they are to help a stranger find one of their own.“
Obryn nodded in agreement.  “I’m sorry, my friend, it is all I have.”
“It’s okay.” Clay nodded, glancing at the newsfeed behind Hoefer, wondering how it would end up spinning the death of a yorohito at the supposedly neutral Hotel Fontaine. “I’ve got a pretty good idea where to look.”



  
CHAPTER 14.	(SCRUB)
The air taxi had dropped him off on the edge of a large canal that ran through the center of the metroplex, and the driver explained that flying any closer to his destination was far too dangerous. With apologies, he still took the full fare before pointing Clay in the direction he wanted to go and wished him luck.
“Where are you, Clay?” Ness asked through the comm. 
“A few kilometers north of the city. Apparently, about to wander a few more through a frozen desert.”
“You mean a war zone. That’s no place you want to be.”
“Maybe not. Distract me then. What are the kids doing these days.”
“Well, Tariq Saleh is now head of Tachibana’s Shadow.  That’s one I didn’t see coming.”
“Max Van Der Jilk assured me that Tachibana’s Shadow collapsed.  He seemed pretty sure of it. That name, Tariq Saleh came up yesterday though, who is he?”
“Not a guy you want to meet. They say he’s a ruthless warlord and cold-blooded killer. Possibly former Tokusha who has turned on his masters. There’s always a rumor about him killing some faction leader and taking over their operations, but they never pan out.  I think he’s an urban myth, personally.” 
Clay approached a large dune of discarded scrap, now covered by the elements, reduced to a snow mound with bits of metal jutting out. It was too big to circumnavigate, so he began to climb, struggling to stay on solid footing and not misstep and fall into its depths.
“Prevo and/or Mother Eye are manipulating facts again. Why? To start a war between the factions?”
“Well, it’s working. It’s all a powder keg ready to blow at any minute. You gonna tell me where you’re going?”
“Harba City.”
“You’ve got to be joking.  Why?”
“Mother Eye pinned Hoefer’s murder on me, but claimed I was working for the Eyes of Baal.  Last night, she blamed the Eyes of Baal for the break-in at Klein’s. From what I understand, that would be very out of character for the Eyes of Baal, since they rarely, if ever, leave Harba City.  I think she’s trying to direct me there.”
“At the same time, she’s also trying to direct you to a prison cell in a government correction center?  You realize, the one thing all the factions have in common is, none of them trust Mother Eye?”
“Yet she persists.  That should tell you something about this planet.”
“Just wait, you’re about to visit the garden spot.”
“You’re sure?  This place looks more like an abandoned war zone than part of the metroplex.”
“You’re still on the outskirts. You’ll see it once you get over the defensive ridge. You’re sure you want to do this?  Harba City isn’t exactly friendly to off-worlders.”
Clay’s right foot sank into snow, and he fell two feet into the drift before catching himself on an outcropping of pipe. He struggled for a moment, then finally got a foothold and hauled himself back up on top of the dune.  “Not a lot of choice. Unless you really think poking around Low Town, as Obryn suggested, is a better idea.”
“That wasn’t a suggestion. It was a trap. There’s no way she or Hēi Gēzi are hiding out there.”
“How do you figure?”
“Low Town residents hate outsiders. They’d rip a Tokusha to shreds if they caught one there.  Maybe you fell for the helpless, doe-eyed, blonde shtick, but nobody in Low Town would.  It’s literally the last place I would expect to find her.” 
“What are the odds she’s in Harba City?”
“Fit’s her profile, anyway. Nearly a hundred thousand people live there, and half of them are on the run from something. It’s a great place to disappear. ‘Eyes of Baal’ administers it. They’re reasonable enough until you cross them.  The government and local security won’t go into the city, so the residents keep things relatively orderly, if completely lawless.”
Clay climbed the last meter of smashed plascrete to the top of the dune. In the distance, across a wasteland of crumbled buildings and wrecked machinery, were ten square blocks of makeshift structures, piled 75 meters high and so close together that they appeared to be one giant structure, similar to what Clay remembered as a late-20th- and early-21st-century Brazilian favela. 
Clay started down the far side of the rubble pile. He took note of the vidcams the local authorities would have on alert for him and pulled his hat low. In the distance, he heard gunfire, explosions, and screams of rage, fear, and agony.  “You said, ‘relatively orderly.’“
He could picture Ness Grinning. “That’s just local street gangs defending their territories in the wastelands. They run drugs for the minor factions and make the government generally look ineffective in exchange for improvised explosives and low-powered ballistic guns. Stay out of the crossfire and try not to look scary; you’ll probably be okay.”
Clay checked the load in his sidearm and shoved it back in his shoulder holster. Then, he patted the inside of his jacket, carrying the remains of his arsenal: Two stuncaps, two smokers, a stash of credslips, and one vial of Shard with hypogun. Ness would probably argue against him carrying Shard around openly, but Clay decided he needed a peace offering to convince Ursza to trust him.
Clay nodded. “How do you find anything in a place like that?”
“Find an information broker and tell him what you’re looking for. They have a network that runs all through that place. Just knock over the first slimy rock you find,” Ness said. "Watch your back when you do it, though. You’re just as much commodity as customer to them.”
“Get up here as soon as you can. If all goes well, I’ll meet you here on the dune, due south of the city, when I’m done. If it doesn’t, I’ll signal you for a more improvisational exit.”  
He pulled his coat tight around him when he reached the bottom of the pile and started towards the massive structures, crouching as he darted across an abandoned stretch of flat ground. Eventually, he reached the plascrete wall, three meters high, that stretched along one side of the city. It was crumbling in places, making it relatively easy to scale, if necessary. Sliding corrugated metal gateways provided more conventional entrances every few hundred meters. He quickly realized these were not fortifications for defense but merely means of delineating anarchy from order.
He gave one of the gates a gentle tug to open it and stepped inside. The counterweight on the gate caused it to slide gently back into position. He was in a small courtyard with three narrow hallways leading deeper into the structures. It took him a minute to register that these hallways were formerly alleys between buildings and that he was standing in the middle of what used to be a street.
It was no wonder that neither the government nor city law enforcement wanted anything to do with this place. There were no defensible routes where invaders could gain a foothold and hold it, and none of the “streets” were more than a meter across, negating any advantage from heavy arms or armored vehicles.
He picked the passageway that seemed to run the furthest, nearly to the open park area in that quarter of the city. Along the way, he passed several small alleys and chambers. Most were living quarters, though he did spot shops, clinics, temples, and even cafes randomly dotted amongst them. There was no urban planning or organization of any sort. It seemed people just picked any available spot and established their trade there. Desperately needing information, he stepped into a café, which was nothing more than a three-meter-square room with three tables adjacent to a makeshift kitchen, where a surprisingly pleasant aroma of spices emanated. A kindly woman, who appeared to be well over a century old, though probably only half that in reality, set a plate containing flatbread, beans, and some kind of meat in front of him, along with metal cutlery. He gingerly sampled everything, most of which he found palatable, and none of his enhanced biodefenses tripped.
He smiled at the woman in appreciation and placed a credslip on the table. She began to protest when he held up a hand to stop her. “I’m looking for an information broker,” he said as matter-of-factly as he could manage.
“Broker? Well, that depends on the information you’re looking for.”
“I’m trying to find someone.”  He tried to sound non-threatening, then added, “I need their help.”
“Someone who lives here?” She scowled.
“That’s what I’ve been led to believe.”
“You want Dex, then. He knows where everyone is.”  She turned to go.  It was obvious she didn’t trust him and wanted the conversation to end.
“Dex,” Clay repeated.
She pointed down the corridor outside. “Avenue A to the park, immediately turn left. When you hit the park’s edge, turn right, second street.”
“Thank you,” Clay said. He caught the woman examining the credslip to determine if it was genuine.
The temperature dropped rapidly as he walked, and the corridor opened into a large park in the middle of the city, with a view of the sky dozens of meters above. To his surprise, it appeared to be used strictly as a recreational area rather than farmland. As he looked up, he noted various fruits and vegetables being grown on hydroponic shelves on the balconies all along the park’s perimeter, making the entire view seem lusher and more picturesque than it was.
He turned and walked next to the structures along the garden’s edge until he reached the corner. Then, as instructed, he turned right and walked to the second street. This part of the city didn’t look much different from the part he had just left, though the energy was much livelier.  A few people even smiled at him as they passed in the corridor. Finally, he decided he was never going to find Dex on his own.
“Excuse me,” he said to a passing man. “I’m looking for Dex.”
The man froze and stared at Clay, who shrugged and held up a credslip.  The man eyed him curiously, as if he were something he had just found under a rock.  Without saying anything, the man took the money and gestured for Clay to follow. He led him through several adjoining rooms; all seemed to be small, one-room apartments. Clay apologized to each occupant as he passed through. After several turns down narrow corridors, they came to a room tucked neatly beneath a stairway, which gave it a long, sloping ceiling. It contained just a desk and chair, and its walls were lined floor to ceiling with books. A man sat at the desk, apparently reading. The escort pointed to the man and then returned the way he came.
“You’re looking for me?” the man asked. He was in his early thirties, dressed in a brown leather tunic, and had a curious piece of cybernetic hardware affixed to his shaved head. The look was completed by a pair of round, oversized spectacles with reflective lenses, which Clay assumed to be an old-style terminal display.
“That depends. Are you Dex?”
“I’m a dex, yes.”  The man smiled. “Name’s Ren. Ren Sato.”  He stood up, pulled a threadbare blanket aside, and ushered Clay into the next room. It was larger than the previous one. The walls were lined with old two-dimensional photographs and drawings. A worn cot was in one corner, and a large terminal looked very out of place sitting in the other.
Ren made his way to the far side of the room by the terminal, retrieved a canister from a shelf, shook it—revealing the distinct clang of credslips—and placed it on the desk. He then walked around and sat behind the terminal, smiling warmly. “How can I help you today?”
“I need a job done, and I’d like it done by a specific group of people living here in Harba City.”
“Gotcha,” Ren said non-committedly, holding up a hand to stop Clay from going further, and began extracting a cable from the console.
Clay shook his head, confused. “Can you help or not?”
“Don’t know,” Ren shrugged.
Clay smiled and threw a few credslips in the canister. “How about now?”
He paused and looked at Clay with curiosity. “Still don’t know.” Ren shrugged, smiled, and continued unraveling the cable. “But thank you for that.”
“I don’t understand. I was told you could help me.”
Ren arched an eyebrow. “I’m a scrub, boss.” He seemed to laugh as he said it, pointing to the cybernetic device attached to his head.
“I thought you were a dex.” Clay was beginning to lose his patience.
“Most of the best dexes are scrubs.   Give me just a minute.”  He connected the cable to a neural jack behind his left ear, removed his glasses, and powered up the terminal. Instantly, he closed his eyes and smiled broadly as if a wave of pure pleasure had washed over him.
“Okay, here we go. You have a job and want to hire someone in the city. So, what’s the job?”
“I don’t think you need to know that. That’s between them and me.”
“Boss, you don’t know what I need to know. I don’t even know what I need to know. That’s why you have to tell me what the job is.”
“I’ll tell you about the job if you assure me you can find a woman named Ursza Venter or a man named Hēi Gēzi.”
“Of course, I can find them.”
“You weren’t so sure a minute ago.”
“No, I wasn’t sure I could help you a minute ago. I’m still not sure.”
“You’re intentionally being cryptic, now.”
“Sorry, boss. Love to take the piss out of visitors, I guess. We’ll start over. I will help you, but I need to know what your business is.”
 “I assure you, if you’re protecting them, I mean them no harm.”
“There are twenty sensors on you right now, boss, and you just lit up with lies. Are these bad people?”
“You tell me. You’re hooked into that machine. Can’t you pull up everything about them?”
Ren smiled and nodded. “One man’s evil … is another man’s most pious day.”  He arched an eyebrow. “You aren’t exactly a saint yourself, Mr. Sean Clay, Sol Directorate Division 5.” He quickly began reading the information on the screen in front of him. "Wow. This is exciting.”
Clay, amazed at how fast Ren had pulled his cover and hacked Division’s systems, kept his expression neutral. “So, what is this?  Some kind of judgment day?  Only the worthy shall pass?  Are you to be judge and jury?”
“No, nothing so grandiose, Agent Clay. I’m just a scrub trying to keep himself sane when he wakes up and has to live with himself tomorrow.” Ren tapped the implant on his head again and looked at Clay expectantly. “So yes, I know what they are and what you are, and now I just need to know your business.” 
Suddenly, Clay began to understand. “That’s a neural leucotome attached to your frontal cortex, right?”
“Now you get it.”
“As soon as you disconnect from that terminal, it erases all your memory of what you did while you were connected.”
“Wouldn’t last long in this business if I knew too much now, would I?”
“The ultimate in privacy. I’d heard about such things decades ago but never dreamed they were still in practice.”
“Well, hey, I never thought I’d meet a secret agent either, so let’s just get to business, and you tell me what the business is.”
“One question,” Clay said, holding up a finger. “Why do you care what my business is if you’re just going to forget in a few moments?”
“Because a man can’t pray for forgiveness if he doesn’t know he’s sinned against his fellow man. Therefore, I long ago promised my sleeping self that I would turn down the job if I were asked to betray anyone or do anything evil. This way, I know my honor is still intact when I wake up. Well, aside from some rigidly defined gray areas where I will sometimes operate. So, one last time, what’s your business with the girl and the ninja? They’re bad people. You gonna kill them?”
“I’ve been asked to,” Clay said. “As you say, they’re bad people.”
“I don’t know. They’ve never done me harm. They haven’t broken any laws in the city that I know of. Maybe they did evil things for one of their faction friends, but as I said, one man’s evil. You’ve probably killed more people than they have.”
“Fine,” Clay said. “Forget about Hēi Gēzi. Let’s stick with the girl. She’s in trouble. She’s addicted to Shard and probably starting withdrawals. The problem is, her regular supplier is dead, so right now, I’m the only person on this planet who can give her that next fix and keep her alive.”
“And you’re gonna use this fix to get what you want out of her, aren’t you, boss?” Ren clicked his tongue. “I know you’re speaking truth, and I know if I help you, you’ll use her to get to the ninja. I also know she’ll die if I don’t help you. This is creating a huge moral dilemma for me.”
“You and me both. This planet is insane. Why don’t you comfort yourself knowing that the girl is smart? When she finds out I have Shard, she’ll likely send the ninja to kill me for it long before I can harm either of them.”
“There’s still a stain on your soul, even if you took a risk getting it. A few bruises and scary nights don’t absolve you.” Ren closed his eyes and lowered his head. Clay couldn’t tell if it was concentration or prayer. 
“I’m not asking for absolution. I’m not expecting to win. I need to try though.”
Ren opened his eyes. “I suppose I’ll just have to have a little faith that there’s still some light in you, then.”


CHAPTER 15.	(LOTUS)
Talbot is dead. I have the last of his product. I’m in Harba City. My original offer still stands. Please reconsider. We are both running out of time.
Clay must have read the message a hundred times over the past three hours. It was a good offer. As things stood, it was probably the only offer that would save her life, assuming she were still alive, assuming she wanted to live. Ren could only confirm that she was registered as living in the city and had listed a personal terminal for receiving messages. He did not specify a location for her quarters or confirm whether she was in the area. Perhaps this was all futile.
A kindly woman walked by and refilled his glass with more of the amber liquid he had been drinking all night. His biosensors indicated it was a wine infused with many local herbs and flowers. Like the coffee back at the hostel, he found it surprising – objectively bad, with multiple overpowering flavors that did not mesh well together – but knew he could grow to like it given time. While it was strong in flavor, the alcohol content was slightly lower than wine, so he drank it without fear of dulling his reflexes. 
He smiled at the woman and put two credslips on the table. “How much for the rest of the bottle?” He asked.
The woman scoffed and set it down on the table, then scooped up the money and departed to the back room.  Clay watched her leave, wondering if he had offended her. He could have given more if she’d asked.  When he turned back to his bottle, he saw a robed figure standing in the doorway. He was a male in his early thirties, with dark skin and a shaved head. He wore a black, loose-fitting tunic over a fit, muscular frame.
“Good evening,” Clay said, attempting not to sound like he had been caught off guard.
The man said nothing but reached into a fold on his left sleeve, withdrew a round, white flower the size of his palm, and tossed it onto the table next to Clay. Without saying a word, he walked away. 
Clay picked up the flower and regarded it carefully. He was no expert on horticulture, but it seemed familiar to him, most likely of Earth origin. He wondered what story brought it to the ugliest part of the most distant world from its ancestral home.  He called for the woman in the next room and showed her the strange gift he had just received.
She smiled, recognizing it instantly. “Lotus.” She nodded in appreciation. “Pretty one at that. Big for this time of year.”
“A man just stepped in, gave it to me, and walked away. Does it have some significance?”
“I’m no expert. The Buddhists use them, so it probably has some significance to them.”
“Where would I find these Buddhists?”
“Oh, they’re spread out all over. It’s one of the more minor religious factions around here. They grow these in their shrines. I’ve heard each shrine grows a different color.”
Buddhism might make some sense, Clay thought to himself, trying to remember if the ninjas in the old stories were Buddhists.  “Where is the temple where they grow these white ones?”
#
It took Clay nearly 90 minutes to maneuver the labyrinth of corridors and buildings that eventually led him to the Jizo shrine on the city’s west end. Most of this time was spent interpreting directions from the woman at the cafe, who seemed to assume he would understand the combination of coordinates, landmark references, people’s names, and various local terms to describe non-compass directions in a three-dimensional space.
He found the shrine behind a beaded curtain in the back of a small antechamber situated halfway down one of the most trafficked corridors in the quarter. A sign on the wall next to the entrance indicated that the shrine was dedicated to Jizo Bosatsu, a protector of travelers. It was open to all as a place of solace and meditation, regardless of faith, provided they remained respectful.
Clay pushed the beads aside and stepped into the temple itself. It was one of the largest rooms he had encountered in Harba City, and he had not realized how claustrophobic he was beginning to feel until he saw it. The walls were all vertical gardens containing a thick ivy-like vine that bore sweet-smelling white flowers that reminded him of honeysuckle, though they were probably indigenous flora. The center of the room was a sunken, two-diameter pool of muddy water. Floating on the water, he saw dozens, possibly hundreds, of white lotus flowers blooming.
At the room’s far end was a small pergola with two concrete benches flanking a stone statue. Grow lights set to mimic natural daylight, including what was now the last rays of sunset, illuminated the room, giving everything a peaceful glow. He could make out the baby-like features of the statue and the distinctly feminine features of Ursza Venter kneeling on the ground in front of it. He cautiously made his way around the perimeter of the room, careful not to step in the muddy garden, and knelt beside her in an approximation of her submissive pose -- kneeling, sitting on her feet, palms down on her thighs. Eventually, he succumbed to his age and failing joints and opted to sit cross-legged on his rear end instead.
He placed the lotus on the ground before her and waited for a reaction. She stared at it for a few moments as if remembering something. Her eyes were sunken with puffy dark circles under them. Her hands were still, but her fingers were fidgeting. He estimated that limiting her physical discomfort to this single outward expression was taking all her concentration.
“Do you know the significance of that flower?” she asked, her voice trembling slightly. She immediately took a deep breath to focus herself.
“No, I don’t.”
“The lotus grows in mud, rising out of it to bloom. It represents purity and beauty, striving for enlightenment, leaving the worldly mud it was born in. Then, at the end of the day, it closes again and sinks back into the mud for the night, waiting to be reborn again the next day in an ever-repeating cycle.”
“That seems familiar,” Clay said without levity.
“The white lotus represents purity and spirituality. The gift of a white lotus indicates compassion and positive intentions from the giver, as well as a blessing and desire for the recipient to achieve spiritual purity.”
Clay suddenly felt a swelling of sympathy for the girl. He instantly swallowed it on the off chance that she was trying to manipulate him. “We need to start over. I know you’re Ursza Venter. You are, or maybe you were, the handler for an assassin named Hēi Gēzi, and you have a datarod containing plans for something called Keraunos.  My name is Clay. I’m with Sol Directorate, Division 5, and my orders are to retrieve that datarod and return it to Earth.”
“Did you kill Sem Talbot?” Urza asked slowly. Her voice was completely devoid of emotion, and she interrupted him as if she wasn’t paying attention.
“Yes.” Clay nodded. “Yes, I did, but I managed to get hold of a five-day supply of Shard before he died. It’s enough to keep you alive and comfortable until I can get you to Earth, and I believe I have the formula and process to make more once we get there. There’s no antidote or way to break the addiction currently, but they have the best scientists. They can find a way to cure you.”  He wasn’t sure about this, but he needed to offer her something to win her trust.
Ursza still made no indication she was listening to anything he said. “Did you enjoy killing him?”
“Are you even paying attention?” Clay started to raise his voice, but looked at her gaunt face and her sunken eyes and saw how she was struggling to keep from shaking. He instantly calmed himself, searching for patience. “My job sometimes requires me to kill. I try not to put any feeling or emotion into it when I do.”
“But did you enjoy killing Talbot?  You have kind eyes. He does not.”
Clay decided that the girl was on the ragged edge of sanity. He took a chance, removed the hypo containing the Shard from his inside jacket pocket, and placed it on the ground in front of her, next to the flower.
She slowly turned her head and looked him in the eyes for the first time since he sat down. Without speaking, she immediately grabbed the hypo, adjusted the dial on the back, and injected her thigh. Clay saw that she had only dispersed half of the contents. Then she tossed the hypo in Clay’s general direction and closed her eyes.  When she opened them again, they were full of life. The bags and dark circles had disappeared, and the shaking in her fingers had stopped. Her complexion instantly changed to a healthy pink, and a mischievous smile crossed her lips. Clay stared at her, slack-jawed. In fifteen seconds, she had transformed from a wretched waif to a beautiful, vibrant young woman.
“You didn’t answer the question,” she said, still smiling. “Her voice was much smoother now, more confident.”
“Okay, yes. I suppose I did. Especially after seeing what he’s done to you and so many people like you.”
She suddenly seemed sad. “I enjoy killing too much. Doesn’t matter why.”
“Why did you only take a half-dose?  Talbot said there was no way to break the addiction cycle, so trying to wean yourself off isn’t going to help. I told you I have a five-day supply. You don’t need to hurt anymore, I can--”
“Do you have orders to kill Hēi Gēzi?” she asked accusingly.
“Is that why you only took a half-dose?  Is he addicted as well?”  She said nothing, and Clay thought for a moment. “Okay, if you can get Keraunos and Hēi Gēzi quickly, we can leave the system tonight and get you both to Earth before the withdrawals get too bad,” he lied. He may not be able to bring himself to kill the girl, but the ninja would most certainly meet with an unfortunate airlock accident somewhere on the way to the gate station. With luck, he could convince his superiors that she was an unwitting participant in all this, doing it out of addiction and had very little involvement with Keraunos.
She sat, staring at the shrine, deep in thought. Clay could see she was considering it. “Kind eyes show when they’re lying,” Ursza said, shaking her head again. He’d fooled an SI deception tracker on Excipio station. Why wasn’t he fooling this girl?
“Is it Hēi Gēzi? Are you afraid for his safety? Did you betray him, and you’re afraid of what he’ll do to you? I can protect you.”
She stood up. Clay saw the same disappointed mother’s expression from their previous meeting. “I won’t be going with you,” she said with finality.
“What!?”
“I cannot trust you. Everyone who sees Keraunos dies. Those are your orders. Those are my orders. That’s why you have the yin, and I have the yang. One of us is next. Either by your hand or mine.”
“So, you do still have it. What are you going to do, go into hiding?  You understand the Shard is done. Nobody’s making any more. If you had a stockpile, I wouldn’t have found you here detoxing.  You’re going to die if you don’t let me help you.”
She stepped down from the pergola and started walking around the edge of the room towards the exit. “Still work to do. I need Keraunos for that.”
“I don’t understand. What work?”
“This planet is insane. It destroys people. It takes anything with honor or purity and strips it away. It has to be stopped. You should go before it gets you too, while you still have kind eyes.”
“This is crazy. I thought we understood each other. Let me help you. I can protect you. I thought you believed me. I thought we were on the same page. The white lotus, didn’t you mean any of that when you sent it? Good intentions, desire to find purity and rise above?”
“No.” Ursza stopped dead in her tracks. “You gifted the white lotus to me.”
Clay held up right behind her. “Well, yes, but the man in the robes. He gave it to me first.”  Clay’s blood turned cold. She turned, and the two stared at each other for a long moment. "Oh no. You mean, you weren’t the one who sent it?”
Her expression turned to fear, and suddenly, he realized they’d both been set up.


CHAPTER 16.	(INNER CITY)
Clay pulled the sidearm from his holster and leaped in front of Ursza, stopping at the shrine’s entrance. “Okay, just stay behind me, whatever happens,” he whispered. He checked the load on the plaser, moved the beaded curtain aside, and gazed out into the hallway. “Okay, clear,” he said, covering the corridor and motioning for Ursza to move out of the entrance and to the right.
Once in the corridor, he tapped the transmit button on his com. “Improvisational exit,” he said intently, only half-hoping that Ness was awake and monitoring the frequency.
“Roof. Southwest corner, five minutes,” came an immediate reply. 
Clay had little sense of direction but was almost positive they were opposite the side of the city Ness was directing them to. “The tracker. Do you still have it?” he asked calmly.
